    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8821855055335992562">The long awaited sequel to Gene Wolfe’s four-volume classic, <i>The Book of the New Sun</i>. We return to the world of Severian, now the Autarch of Urth, as he leaves the planet on one of the huge spaceships of the alien Hierodules to travel across time and space to face his greatest test, to become the legendary New Sun or die. The strange, rich, original spaceship scenes give w</span>
  <span id="freeText8821855055335992562" style="display:none">The long awaited sequel to Gene Wolfe’s four-volume classic, <i>The Book of the New Sun</i>. We return to the world of Severian, now the Autarch of Urth, as he leaves the planet on one of the huge spaceships of the alien Hierodules to travel across time and space to face his greatest test, to become the legendary New Sun or die. The strange, rich, original spaceship scenes give way to travels in time, wherein Severian revisits times and places which fill in parts of the background of the four-volume work, that will thrill and intrigue particularly all readers of the earlier books. But The Urth of the New Sun is an independent structure all of a piece, an integral masterpiece to shelve beside the classics, one itself.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8821855055335992562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

</script>  </div>

<div id="review_571338678" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571338678" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571338678?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2016-shelf">2016-shelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571338678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14542121877921012561">I knew things were going to get interesting the moment we started the book on the spaceship "Yesod", the ninth branch on the tree of life directly above the root, the kingdom, in the Kabbalah. Yesod is the ship that is outside of time and Maya, and the source of the Sun's renewal and the place where Sevarian must make or break his new covenant, the place of the new foundation for humanity.<br/><br/>Nuts? Hell yeah. There's plenty of crazy going on in the whole series, but the fact we start moving up throu</span>
  <span id="freeText14542121877921012561" style="display:none">I knew things were going to get interesting the moment we started the book on the spaceship "Yesod", the ninth branch on the tree of life directly above the root, the kingdom, in the Kabbalah. Yesod is the ship that is outside of time and Maya, and the source of the Sun's renewal and the place where Sevarian must make or break his new covenant, the place of the new foundation for humanity.<br /><br />Nuts? Hell yeah. There's plenty of crazy going on in the whole series, but the fact we start moving up through the branches to Hod, witnessing the splendor of Tzabaoth, kinda sealed the idea for me, and it didn't really matter if these were aliens outside of time who were once on the same road as humanity until we spread throughout the galaxy and did horrendous things and broke the covenant. The underlying shape of the story is clear even if it isn't actually Severian dealing with angels. ;) <br /><br />Things are also a bit more complicated when we move from the flaming swords of Gabriel and return to earth to deal with the personal issue of fractured times and places making Severian deal with the qualities of Glory, Victory, Beauty, Severity, Mercy, Wisdom, and finally the Crown.<br /><br />Unfortunately for me, I was reading purely for entertainment value, so I didn't actually sit down and track every scene to a branch on the tree, but I picked up on at least 4 or 5 of them, easy. :)<br /><br />Impressive? Yeah, I think so too, and we've even got the whole feel of Slaughterhouse Five, the coming resurrection, the return of the king, and a lot of other hints, too, but let's face it... the story is very odd. Scenes feel all right by themselves, but they often take very odd directions from one another, and I can't quite tell if it's because it's following the Tree regardless of the natural progression of story, or whether the story is just odd for its own sake.<br /><br />Having read the previous four, I'd soooo love to say, outright, that it's fundamentally incomprehensible, but no, I think I've actually found a pretty decent roadmap.<br /><br />I'm impressed, but, not quite engrossed in the tale enough to put in the extra work of truly deciphering it. :) Still! Props.<br /><br />:)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14542121877921012561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2592530818" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2592530818" />
    <a title="David Katzman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1444651-david-katzman"><img alt="David Katzman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399048696p2/1444651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2592530818?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Katzman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Katzman" href="/user/show/1444651-david-katzman">David Katzman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2592530818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16721029433710004629">This review is for all five books in the “New Sun” tetralogy.<br/><br/>What a peculiar series. Bizarre and compelling yet also distant and alien. The language is poetic while the tone tends toward melancholy. As if, perhaps, Edgar Allen Poe had written long-form science fiction novels.<br/><br/>The New Sun series, is not for those who demand their fantasy or science fiction served on a platter, spoon fed. Wolfe has written a story that is ambiguous, mysterious and falls in an undefinable land between myth, fantasy </span>
  <span id="freeText16721029433710004629" style="display:none">This review is for all five books in the “New Sun” tetralogy.<br /><br />What a peculiar series. Bizarre and compelling yet also distant and alien. The language is poetic while the tone tends toward melancholy. As if, perhaps, Edgar Allen Poe had written long-form science fiction novels.<br /><br />The New Sun series, is not for those who demand their fantasy or science fiction served on a platter, spoon fed. Wolfe has written a story that is ambiguous, mysterious and falls in an undefinable land between myth, fantasy and science fiction. You might find it experimental in style as compared to mainstream fantasy. The main character is often unsympathetic and unrelatable, yet he remains fascinating and intriguing. Plot threads are seemingly abandoned only to turn up as central events in later books. Periodically, inexplicable experiences are explained only to open up new unknowns. The New Sun series manages to be both satisfying and deeply unsatisfying simultaneously.<br /><br />The story follows a youth named Severian who begins life in the Guild of the Torturers (which applies the government’s “justice” to prisoners) until he eventually breaks away, has many adventures, grows into adulthood, travels through time and the universe, and eventually attempts to save the Earth from its dying sun. Hence the title the “New Sun.”<br /><br />The setting of the series is itself rather ambiguous. Initially it appears to be a medieval level fantasy with some elements of magic. Over the course of the novels, it becomes more clear that there are aliens and genetic mutants present and aspects of high-level technology. Some of what appears to be magical, may in fact be advanced technology. However, at many points, it is undefinable and may in fact be supernatural. <br /><br />The story is told in first person at all times, from the perspective of Severian who has an eidetic memory. Our understanding of what goes on around him is restricted by his own limitations and often confused knowledge. He also makes unpredictable decisions and his descriptions of his own motivations is sometimes less than enlightening. “Enigmatic” is a good word for him.<br /><br />The journey of Severian is thematically in some ways Biblical. I’ve read that some aspects of the series are Kabbalistic, but I’m less familiar with that than the more obvious stories of a religious savior whether that be Christ, Osiris, Vishnu or other mythological characters of rebirth and redemption. Severian while often struggling to be moral and at times achieving a noble perspective, at other times is selfish or restricted by his perspective. As a justifier of torture, he falls short of a being a clear hero, but he does evolve as the story continues. If he is metaphorically a Christ figure, it’s a gnostic figure and a gnostic perspective definitely fits the exotic journey of Severian. The fifth book, the semi-conclusion to the story, is perhaps the most mysterious and gnostic of all the stories wherein Severian in some way arrives at his final destiny. There are numerous thematic threads that can be teased out of the series, but the most obvious central one to me was that of judgement. As a member of the Torturer’s Guild, Severian begins his journey administering the judgement of others. As time goes on, he himself is repeatedly judged for his actions as well as judging others. Yet perhaps the most damning judgement he inflicts is upon himself. Do we excuse our own actions because we see no “choice” in the matter? Are some individuals oblivious to their own selfishness or do they even revel in their greed as President Trump seems to? I was just following orders, said the Nazi guard. <br /><br />Wolfe’s literary finesse holds the entire work together. His linguistic prowess keeps the story riveting even while it’s confusing. He stays true to a limited first-person perspective, and is consistently true to the conflicted undefinable personality of the main character. In that sense, it’s a true work of art. The New Sun series is not likely to appeal to those who dabble in fantasy or sci fi, nor is it for those who prefer work that is cut and dried. However, if your taste runs to the fantastical with appreciation for an experimental approach, then this is highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16721029433710004629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15525684082206508253" style="display:none">Well, I've given Gene Wolfe a fair shot.<br /><br />This is the fifth volume of (and really more of a coda to) his mammoth opus, <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> - the story of Severian; torturer, soldier, ruler, prophet and savior of his planet. While critics and fans alike rave about his experimental brand of narration, I found this particular installment annoyingly hallucinogenic. Here's the guy on the couch in college, 2 AM, stoned out of his mind, Jedi death-grip on your forearm: "Dude, you gotta hear this. Just let me finish. Gonna blow your mind."<br /><br />Okay. Only tomorrow we both know it's going to be all, "Hey, what did I say?" And you're going to wind up resenting, yet again, the way these psychedelic sages manage to forgive themselves the memory of half the crap they spend so much time forbidding you to forget.<br /></span>

Since that time I have often pondered on that sight, here beside the all-devouring sea.   <span id="freeText9645579726009101426" style="display:none">“There the stars swarmed still, but it seemed to me they formed a great disk in the sky, and when I looked at the edges of that disk, I saw they were streaked and old. Since that time I have often pondered on that sight, here beside the all-devouring sea. It is said that so great a thing is the universe that no one can see it as it is, but only as it was…”<br /><i>The Urth of the New Sun</i> begins as a space opera – a turbulent journey along the corridors of space and time… <br />Then the monstrous spaceship arrives at the planet Yesod and there <b>Gene Wolfe</b> takes a stab at cabbala following the formula ‘as above so below’. In the Tree of Life Yesod is the sephirah right above and nearest to Malkuth: the kingdom or our material world. And there Severian is being judged by Zadkiel – the archangel of freedom, benevolence and mercy – and at last found worthy.<br />“What you call time is made up of such waves, and as the waves you saw existed in the water, so time exists in matter. The waves march toward the beach, but if you were to cast a pebble into the water, new waves, a hundredth or a thousandth the strength of the old, would run out to sea, and the waves there would feel them.”<br />On returning to Urth Severian discovers that he arrived somewhat in the past so there is a smart time loop and he starts working miracles not unlike those in the New Testament and the story becomes unintentional or, maybe, intentional fun.<br />And eventually the book ends up as a flowery mockery of mythologisation of earthly events and history.</span>

<div id="review_115182462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115182462" />
    <a title="Paul  Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry"><img alt="Paul  Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606145814p2/4107783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115182462?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul  Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul  Perry" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry">Paul  Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780708882689" href="/book/show/3422595-the-urth-of-the-new-sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=male-author">male-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=epic">epic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115182462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9583994799220813680">This is my second read of this, often referred to as a coda to Wolfe's Book of the New Sun rather than a true fifth volume, and I enjoyed it far more this time. I still think that it isn't quite up to the same level of magnificence as those books, but it is a different creature and great in its own right.<br/><br/>This tale follows Severian - again through his own journals - after he has become the Autarch, the ruler of the empire and (possibly) the rightful although not actual ruler of a fractured planet</span>
  <span id="freeText9583994799220813680" style="display:none">This is my second read of this, often referred to as a coda to Wolfe's Book of the New Sun rather than a true fifth volume, and I enjoyed it far more this time. I still think that it isn't quite up to the same level of magnificence as those books, but it is a different creature and great in its own right.<br /><br />This tale follows Severian - again through his own journals - after he has become the Autarch, the ruler of the empire and (possibly) the rightful although not actual ruler of a fractured planet Urth, with a fractious humanity dwindling under a dying sun, as he travels to the world of Ysod to stand trial for humanity and, if he passes, gain the ability to bring the New Sun to return full life to Urth.<br /><br />This does contain similarities to the previous books - advanced technology, the working of which having been long forgotten, appears as magic, although some still seem to understand it and it is sometimes explained in curious (arguable unnecessarily expositionary) metaphor. It is a picaresque narrative, Severian traveling across space (and time)before returning to journey in his own world, at different times. However, the myth-making aspect that was a lesser part of the earlier books is here far more dominant; this reads often like some future gita or mythic tale explaining how the world came to be what it is, with all the strange metaphor and lack of clarity and occasional confusion you would expect in such a work. It is novel to allow to wash over you like the waters of Ocean, to absorb rather than fully understand.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9583994799220813680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who continue to read a series &amp; just don't know when to stop. Like me.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer13076190041376442790"><b>If you enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23069.Gene_Wolfe' title='Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gene Wolfe's</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968868.The_Book_of_the_New_Sun' title='The Book of the New Sun by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book of the New Sun</a> series which includes, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1234310.The_Shadow_of_the_Torturer' title='The Shadow of the Torturer by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Shadow of the Torturer</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776459.The_Claw_of_the_Conciliator__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun___2_' title='The Claw of the Conciliator (The Book of the New Sun, #2) by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Claw of the Conciliator</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/729728.The_Sword_of_the_Lictor' title='The Sword of the Lictor by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sword of the Lictor</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/299758.The_Citadel_of_the_Autarch__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun__4_' title='The Citadel of the Autarch (The Book of the New Sun #4) by Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Citadel of the Autarch</a>, DON'T EVER READ THIS!!!!! <br/><br/>This is supposed to be the 5th book in the series, written much later, and I don't know what on Earth <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23069.Gene_Wolfe' title='Gene Wolfe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gene</a> was thinking!?!?!? This book has nearly NOTHING to do with the rest of the series and I've determined he must've been smoking some bad shi+ to come up with this horrible idea of </b></span>
  <span id="freeText13076190041376442790" style="display:none"><b>If you enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23069.Gene_Wolfe" title="Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">Gene Wolfe's</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968868.The_Book_of_the_New_Sun" title="The Book of the New Sun by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of the New Sun</a> series which includes, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1234310.The_Shadow_of_the_Torturer" title="The Shadow of the Torturer by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shadow of the Torturer</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776459.The_Claw_of_the_Conciliator__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun___2_" title="The Claw of the Conciliator (The Book of the New Sun, #2) by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Claw of the Conciliator</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/729728.The_Sword_of_the_Lictor" title="The Sword of the Lictor by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sword of the Lictor</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/299758.The_Citadel_of_the_Autarch__The_Book_of_the_New_Sun__4_" title="The Citadel of the Autarch (The Book of the New Sun #4) by Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Citadel of the Autarch</a>, DON'T EVER READ THIS!!!!! <br /><br />This is supposed to be the 5th book in the series, written much later, and I don't know what on Earth <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/23069.Gene_Wolfe" title="Gene Wolfe" rel="nofollow noopener">Gene</a> was thinking!?!?!? This book has nearly NOTHING to do with the rest of the series and I've determined he must've been smoking some bad shi+ to come up with this horrible idea of a book.<br /><br />I have never been so disappointed in the continuation of a series ever before.<br /><br />I originally gave this book 2 stars ONLY because he is such an amazing writer and thought it had to be acknowledged as so, but after trying to read it again its just fargin awful.<br /><br />Save yourself a HUGE disappointment and skip this one and go read ANY of his far superior books.<br /><br />If I were him, I would disown this book.</b><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_445474235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/445474235" />
    <a title="Terry " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1262683-terry"><img alt="Terry " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316628758p2/1262683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445474235?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terry " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terry " href="/user/show/1262683-terry">Terry </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=dying-earth">dying-earth</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445474235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4396147396673286292">4 – 4.5 stars<br/><br/>The final segment of the New Sun series, whether you consider it a somewhat separate coda to the main four books or simply volume five of Severian’s story, is a satisfying conclusion to all that has led up to it and trust me when I say that for Wolfe that is a surprising turn of events. Don’t get me wrong, plenty of mysteries and unanswered questions remain, but there is a sense of completion and clarity that is often not apparent in Wolfe’s work. I actually found, on this re-read a</span>
  <span id="freeText4396147396673286292" style="display:none">4 – 4.5 stars<br /><br />The final segment of the New Sun series, whether you consider it a somewhat separate coda to the main four books or simply volume five of Severian’s story, is a satisfying conclusion to all that has led up to it and trust me when I say that for Wolfe that is a surprising turn of events. Don’t get me wrong, plenty of mysteries and unanswered questions remain, but there is a sense of completion and clarity that is often not apparent in Wolfe’s work. I actually found, on this re-read at least, that nearly all of the big mysteries were resolved to my satisfaction as the story progressed, though I was perhaps reading the text more closely than I had done before. <br /><br />We last left Severian on the edge of taking up the mantle of Autarch with his journey of discovery and growth coming to an end just as the greater quest to bring about the New Sun was literally rising upon the horizon. In ‘Urth’ we see Severian take up that quest in earnest as we skip over the years of his rulership of the Commonwealth and join him as he embarks upon a solar sail ship to be judged as “the epitome of Urth” by the angelic extraterrestrials that have been overseeing the growth and development of humanity. The first main segment of the story takes place on this ship, a gigantic vessel that plys the spaceways not only between planets, but also between realities, as Severian is ferried from the dying universe of Briah to the higher one of Yesod (terms taken from kabbalistic mysticism and which show some of the ideas that Wolfe is playing with in his conception of Severian’s universe).<br /><br />I found the ship fascinating in itself as we witness the strange nature of its composition and inhabitants as the story unfolds. I also have to admit to being partial to the whole concept of solar sail starships, and so I was obviously taken by this star-faring vessel with masts that dwarf towers and sails that could seemingly cover a continent. The complexities and ambiguities of space travel (and its intersection with time travel) are played with to great effect and we see how the multitudinous crew of this cavernous vessel ply the star ways in and out of time, perhaps arriving on their homeworld at a point generations after (or before) they initially signed on. We also see the ‘magical’ implications of the solar sails and their connection to the mirrors of Father Inire from the earlier books: sailors on this ship need to be wary of dangerous people or creatures their own ship inadvertently brings aboard, nevermind any dangers they may face on one of their extra-solar ports of call.<br /><br />The next section of the story covers Severian’s ‘trial’ on the paradaisaical world of Yesod. It is also where Severian begins to truly understand the implications of his role as possible bringer of the New Sun for the world of Urth; should he succeed he will not simply be a saviour as he might have liked to believe, but a destroyer as well. If there wasn’t enough timey-wimey stuff for you before hold onto your seats because we’re in for a whackload of strange adventures throughout time and relative dimensions in space as we enter the final section of the book in which Severian returns to his homeworld. It is here that Severian truly comes into his power and we learn the secrets that lay behind the abilities previously attributed to the relic known as the Claw of the Conciliator. As we see time wrapped into an escherian knot around the torturer’s life many mysteries are resolved, or at least clarified, and we have the chance to revisit some familiar people and places and see them with new eyes. Ultimately we witness the birth of the Conciliator and begin to more fully appreciate the cyclical nature of Severian’s life. <br /><br />For my money this is definitely one of Wolfe's best series (only matched in my eyes by the Latro books and the Wizard Knight duology). Perseverence can really pay off with these ones, and who wouldn't want a sword and sorcery adventure melded with a star-faring space opera with the fate of the earth (oops, I mean urth) in the hands of an erstwhile torturer?</span>
  <a data-text-id="4396147396673286292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3159595834" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3159595834" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3159595834?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3159595834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8855528187370410162">This is a fine ending to this most wonderful series, but it does lack some of the gritty polish and bemusing weirdness of its four predecessors. Perhaps it is just familiarity with the world of Severian the Torturer that makes this one a little less insane, and a good portion of the book is taken up by crazy battles on a time-bending space<i>ship</i>, a literal galleon with a thousand sails whisking our "hero" to the universe Yesod to find the New Sun. None of that will mean anything to you if you have</span>
  <span id="freeText8855528187370410162" style="display:none">This is a fine ending to this most wonderful series, but it does lack some of the gritty polish and bemusing weirdness of its four predecessors. Perhaps it is just familiarity with the world of Severian the Torturer that makes this one a little less insane, and a good portion of the book is taken up by crazy battles on a time-bending space<i>ship</i>, a literal galleon with a thousand sails whisking our "hero" to the universe Yesod to find the New Sun. None of that will mean anything to you if you haven't read the other books, but you really should. They are the most wonderfully written, strange, and mystifying works of science fiction or fantasy you are likely ever to read.<br />This one, for all its weird wonder, is often hard to follow since time and physics are bent out of recognition at times in the story and nothing ever stays as it should be, in a natural sense. But, still, it's way better than most shit in this genre you'll read, so, there's that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8855528187370410162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156800055" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156800055" />
    <a title="Nick Tramdack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5107229-nick-tramdack"><img alt="Nick Tramdack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299807366p2/5107229.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156800055?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Tramdack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Tramdack" href="/user/show/5107229-nick-tramdack">Nick Tramdack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156800055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15673668529023055753">"Declan wished to know how Urth would fare when the New Sun came; and I, understanding little more than he did himself, drew upon Dr. Talos's play, never thinking that in a time yet to come Dr. Talos's play would be drawn from my words."<br/><br/>A hall of mirrors, a recognition over immense gulfs of time and space, a hero's journey that's perpetually refreshes and corrects itself and encounters its own traces. "I am not the first Severian," our hero said in The Citadel of the Autarch; it takes Wolfe full</span>
  <span id="freeText15673668529023055753" style="display:none">"Declan wished to know how Urth would fare when the New Sun came; and I, understanding little more than he did himself, drew upon Dr. Talos's play, never thinking that in a time yet to come Dr. Talos's play would be drawn from my words."<br /><br />A hall of mirrors, a recognition over immense gulfs of time and space, a hero's journey that's perpetually refreshes and corrects itself and encounters its own traces. "I am not the first Severian," our hero said in The Citadel of the Autarch; it takes Wolfe fully another novel to begin to answer the questions that occur once the chills fade and you say, "cool, but... how that could actually, like, HAPPEN?"<br /><br />Answer: parallel universes, timeloops, spaceships, miracles, memories, supernatural powers, "Eschatology and Genesis". Wolfe definitely tries his damndest to explain, and you WILL finally get some answers in this book. But more questions always appear and in the end the waters are so muddy it's impossible to come to final answers about the complicated continuity revolving around Severian's multiple deaths and mysterious transfigurations.<br /><br />We usually ask fiction to answer all our questions by the end. Ambiguity is failure. Reading Wolfe teaches you what it would be like if this wasn't the case, if events were ultimately unexplainable because they were always mysterious and overdetermined and distorted by ignorance and illusion. In short, if literature actually managed to make itself resemble life. <br /><br />(In a fucking awesome science fantasy setting, let's not forget.)<br /><br />The capstone to the greatest SF series of the century. <br /><br />"It's beyond review" - Max Bane.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="15673668529023055753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_239472315" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/239472315" />
    <a title="Jeroen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7044100-jeroen"><img alt="Jeroen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322229077p2/7044100.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239472315?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeroen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeroen" href="/user/show/7044100-jeroen">Jeroen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7044100-jeroen?shelf=fantasy-low">fantasy-low</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7044100-jeroen?shelf=science-fiction-soft">science-fiction-soft</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239472315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9561018099874288157">I have a problem with all Wolfe's books I have read so far: they make me feel dumb. This can be attributed to two, maybe three things.<br/>(1) Wolfe meticulously crafts his stories so that they tread beyond normal "...and then this happened, then this and then this!" narratives. That is to say, this is <i>exactly</i> what he does in the Book of the New Sun, but in such a fashion that I rarely have a moment to breathe in between passages. Everything flows to and fro, which makes this a very exhausting and ch</span>
  <span id="freeText9561018099874288157" style="display:none">I have a problem with all Wolfe's books I have read so far: they make me feel dumb. This can be attributed to two, maybe three things.<br />(1) Wolfe meticulously crafts his stories so that they tread beyond normal "...and then this happened, then this and then this!" narratives. That is to say, this is <i>exactly</i> what he does in the Book of the New Sun, but in such a fashion that I rarely have a moment to breathe in between passages. Everything flows to and fro, which makes this a very exhausting and challenging read.<br />(2) What is true? Wolfe's use of unreliable narration has been discussed to much extent, so I dare not even try to begin to describe it here. Suffice it to say that the Urth of the New Sun gives (unreliable?) answers to questions raised in the previous novels, but that it also raises just as many if not more.<br />(3) I am stupid and not worthy of understanding.<br /><br />So, what do we actually have here? This so-called coda to the Book of the New Sun fluently continues Severian's quest, be it with a switch from the fantasy with subtle sci-fi nuances here and there in its predecessors to the inverse of this. Abandoned civilisations? Check, but these happen to live on a spaceship. Weird creatures? Check, but they are not what they seem. Weird people? Check, but they are also not who they seem. A really weird finale? Check. Well, actually, double-check, because I could not quite wrap my head around it at first. This is something typical of this series, I now finally understand, having read all five novels: your internal gears should remain active long after having finished one of them. There is much to be discovered as not all is disclosed—which is exactly what this kind of fiction is about. At least, to me it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9561018099874288157" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87144826" />
    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2442753-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277854381p2/2442753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87144826?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/2442753-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2442753-nicole?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87144826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10973641122658945196">This might have gotten 5 stars had I read it right after the other four volumes of the series - this book frequently hearkens back to events in earlier books, and I didn't always remember them. This is partly because the books are JAM PACKED with events and characters, and partly because of Wolfe's writing style. <br/><br/>About that style: some authors telegraph "watch carefully! important stuff coming up!" and their work is pretty easy to skim as a result. Wolfe does the exact opposite, deliberately obf</span>
  <span id="freeText10973641122658945196" style="display:none">This might have gotten 5 stars had I read it right after the other four volumes of the series - this book frequently hearkens back to events in earlier books, and I didn't always remember them. This is partly because the books are JAM PACKED with events and characters, and partly because of Wolfe's writing style. <br /><br />About that style: some authors telegraph "watch carefully! important stuff coming up!" and their work is pretty easy to skim as a result. Wolfe does the exact opposite, deliberately obfuscating the importance of events and making the reader infer the characters' emotions and motivations from their speech and actions. He even comes out and SAYS as much (through Severian). This makes the books tough to follow at times, but more interesting in the long run.<br /><br />Liked this book a lot, although I couldn't quite make sense of the Apu Punchau denouement which seems extremely crucial. As I said with the others, I think I might get more out of this with future re-reads. What I'd really like is to discuss it in the context of a class, but instead I might have to opt for reading messageboards and seeing other people's interpretations.<br /><br />A bit afraid to read anything else by Gene Wolfe. It's hard to imagine that other books he's written will live up to this series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10973641122658945196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_95244000" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95244000" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1564654-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222373756p2/1564654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/95244000?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/1564654-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1564654-mark?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1564654-mark?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95244000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7732735544039330805"><i>The Urth of the New Sun</i> is considered--even by the author--to be a coda to the <i>Book of the New Sun</i> tetralogy, rather than a full-fledged fifth book of the series. I suppose this is because it differs considerably in tone from the four books that make up the <i>Book</i>. As the author tells it, he hadn't planned to write this fifth book until his publisher insisted, based on the assumption that Wolfe's readers would want to know how Severian's quest to bring the New Sun back to Urth went.<br/><br/>Though many asp</span>
  <span id="freeText7732735544039330805" style="display:none"><i>The Urth of the New Sun</i> is considered--even by the author--to be a coda to the <i>Book of the New Sun</i> tetralogy, rather than a full-fledged fifth book of the series. I suppose this is because it differs considerably in tone from the four books that make up the <i>Book</i>. As the author tells it, he hadn't planned to write this fifth book until his publisher insisted, based on the assumption that Wolfe's readers would want to know how Severian's quest to bring the New Sun back to Urth went.<br /><br />Though many aspects of the <i>Book of the New Sun</i> confused me (and it seems I'm not alone in this), these four books have stayed with me since I read them a few years ago, and as time goes on I've realized that, even though I didn't fully comprehend the various complexities of the story, its hold on me has only strengthened with time. Thus was I compelled to read this fifth book, even though I've heard that many of the original tetralogy's fans were disappointed by it.<br /><br />I can see why. The first section follows Severian on his journey to Yesod in a vast, seven-sided ship, and is nothing like the fascinating environment of Urth that serves as the setting for the first four books. When Severian finally returns to Urth (about halfway through the book) I felt some of the old magic from the series returning, and enjoyed that part immensely. But as the end of the book neared, Severian began slipping through time, which added a dreamlike quality to the book that I didn't care for. It added a feeling of unrealness (and, subsequently, triviality) to the proceedings, which is something that, despite how alien the events in the first four books sometimes seemed, rarely occurred in the earlier installments. <br /><br />However, I'm very glad I read the book because it did provide me with answers to some (but not all) of the lingering questions I had upon finishing the tetralogy. Its ending, despite being far from categorical, was more satisfying than the "end" provided by <i>The Citadel of the Autarch</i>. And Wolfe's beautiful use of language continued unabated in this volume, which is a large part of why the tetralogy has stuck with me this long. <br /><br />I look forward to diving into Wolfe's <i>Book of the Long Sun</i> sometime soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7732735544039330805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2110766771" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2110766771" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110766771?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18804522-the-urth-of-the-new-sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110766771" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8115368256551221991">This was the last book in the series <i>The Book of the New Sun</i>. For the most part, I really liked it, and I might even have rated it higher than the four stars I’ve consistently given the other books in this series. However, I thought it went off the rails a bit toward the end.<br/><br/>It gave me the answers I was looking for in terms of what happened after the end of the fourth book, and I enjoyed the story it told. Then, without giving anything away, it shed new light on many of the events from those fir</span>
  <span id="freeText8115368256551221991" style="display:none">This was the last book in the series <i>The Book of the New Sun</i>. For the most part, I really liked it, and I might even have rated it higher than the four stars I’ve consistently given the other books in this series. However, I thought it went off the rails a bit toward the end.<br /><br />It gave me the answers I was looking for in terms of what happened after the end of the fourth book, and I enjoyed the story it told. Then, without giving anything away, it shed new light on many of the events from those first four books, and gave more meaning to them, and I also really enjoyed that. Some things were spelled out, but others were quite a bit more subtle, and I enjoyed catching the various references.<br /><br />My problem was that I thought the author went too far with it by the end. Just as I was admiring the cleverness, he took things a few steps further. I felt like, in an attempt to keep things twisty and complicated, he robbed it of some of the meaningfulness. Kind of like a cook who can’t stop tweaking his recipe until it doesn’t taste quite right anymore, or a painter who keeps adding “just a bit more” to his design until it’s no longer quite as pleasing to the eye.<br /><br />A smaller complaint I had with the series in general is that, since the story centers around Severian, there are some characters who play a large role in the series but for whom we don’t get much closure because their paths diverge. There was one character in particular that I really wanted more follow-up on, especially considering how often he was remembered and referenced in Severian’s narrative. <br /><br />Over all, though, I enjoyed this series quite a lot. I liked that it wasn’t simple or straight forward, and I liked its unique (in my experience) blend of some of the best elements from both science fiction and fantasy. For now, there are many other authors and books I’m interested in trying, but I’d like to cycle back around to give Wolfe another try sometime down the road.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8115368256551221991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812558173" href="/book/show/1404051.The_Urth_of_the_New_Sun">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer249506932" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10049744752381796153">Reading this without having read <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> is likely to result in confusion. Reading this without having read <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> <i>very recently</i> ain't much better. <i>Urth</i> is tightly tied to characters and events of the previous four volumes and provides no handholding for those who haven't read them. (For me, it has been a decade.) This is not an "obvious" coda: according to <a href='http://www.irosf.com/q/zine/article/10536' rel='nofollow noopener'>this discussion</a> <i>Urth</i> exists mainly to keep a Polyanna epilogue out of <i>Book of the New Sun</i>. So it would be e</span>
  <span id="freeText10049744752381796153" style="display:none">Reading this without having read <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> is likely to result in confusion. Reading this without having read <i>The Book of the New Sun</i> <i>very recently</i> ain't much better. <i>Urth</i> is tightly tied to characters and events of the previous four volumes and provides no handholding for those who haven't read them. (For me, it has been a decade.) This is not an "obvious" coda: according to <a href="http://www.irosf.com/q/zine/article/10536" rel="nofollow noopener">this discussion</a> <i>Urth</i> exists mainly to keep a Polyanna epilogue out of <i>Book of the New Sun</i>. So it would be easier to ignore it, and the disruption it causes to the earlier work. Then I might as well advocate ignoring <i>Book of the New Sun</i> as well, which certainly was neither an easy work nor one whose latter parts left its earlier parts alone. So: <i>The Urth of the New Sun</i> is much in the vein of its predecessor. The narrator remains dry and distant, as if he cannot recall how it felt to be the Severian whose activities he is describing. The symbolism is thick on the ground, and whether it has significance other than window-dressing is hard to discern. I do not think I can pass judgement, recommend or disrecommend, without sitting down and again making my way (slowly, carefully!) through the entire work. For some, that is a recommendation in itself--for others, sufficient warning.<br /></span>

<div id="review_624869673" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/624869673" />
    <a title="Phil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5174391-phil"><img alt="Phil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301102223p2/5174391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/624869673?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phil" href="/user/show/5174391-phil">Phil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5174391-phil?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer624869673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11305520856630614184">In this follow-up to the Book of the New Sun, Severian, the Autarch of the Commonwealth of Urth, journeys deep into space. His goal is to acquire the New Sun, a star/energy source/person (or perhaps all of these), which is capable of replacing his planet's dying sun. While his success will ensure the survival of his planet, it carries with it a terrible price.<br/><br/>Much like the preceding four volumes that comprise The Book of the New Sun, I enjoyed this book but I do not feel like I entirely comprehe</span>
  <span id="freeText11305520856630614184" style="display:none">In this follow-up to the Book of the New Sun, Severian, the Autarch of the Commonwealth of Urth, journeys deep into space. His goal is to acquire the New Sun, a star/energy source/person (or perhaps all of these), which is capable of replacing his planet's dying sun. While his success will ensure the survival of his planet, it carries with it a terrible price.<br /><br />Much like the preceding four volumes that comprise The Book of the New Sun, I enjoyed this book but I do not feel like I entirely comprehended it. The imagery throughout the book is fanciful and layered. The narrative also has a mythic cast that reminded me a bit of Frank Herbert's Dune Messiah. Wolfe was apparently a devout Catholic and allusions are hidden in the text, but the alienness of the characters and events makes the story feel closer to esoteric Eastern mysticism than familiar Christian biblical scripture.<br /><br />Adding more layers to the puzzle is the fact that Severian has absorbed the memories (and perhaps identities) of previous Autarchs, his interstellar journey appears to involve time dilation, and even after returning to Urth he encounters and reunites with various characters at different non-chronological points in their lives.<br /><br />Not only is the story dense and cryptic, it is related by a narrator who is both unreliable and somewhat aloof from events. The reader is often told that he wept or contemplated suicide after some bleak or unexpected turn of events, but everything is related with a baffling degree of clinical dispassion. Severian is basically a messiah figure, but one that is not to be entirely trusted.<br /><br />Whether or not a reader will enjoy this book depends a great deal on their tolerance for confusion. If vaguery and cryptic dialogue aren't turn-offs, there's a lot to enjoy here. And if you like re-reading and puzzle-solving, this series rewards it. But be certain to read this book soon after The Book of the New Sun, when it's fresh in your mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11305520856630614184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3062044526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3062044526" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8255719-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332087637p2/8255719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3062044526?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/8255719-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3062044526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13618140280392210855">Depending on who you ask, this is either the fourth or the fifth volume in the author’s acclaimed Book of the New Sun series. The blurb on this website has it as the former, but Wikipedia seems to think that there was at least a tetralogy before this one, which acts as a sort of coda. The others are still in print together whereas this one was a little harder to find. I found this edition in a bookshop while on holiday in Canada — outside of the canonical standards, it’s unusual to stumble acros</span>
  <span id="freeText13618140280392210855" style="display:none">Depending on who you ask, this is either the fourth or the fifth volume in the author’s acclaimed Book of the New Sun series. The blurb on this website has it as the former, but Wikipedia seems to think that there was at least a tetralogy before this one, which acts as a sort of coda. The others are still in print together whereas this one was a little harder to find. I found this edition in a bookshop while on holiday in Canada — outside of the canonical standards, it’s unusual to stumble across any Wolfe at all in British bookshops. No doubt some of this elusiveness is something the author would have enjoyed, although at the same time I do wish it was easier to buy his stuff.<br /><br />This is pretty good. It doesn’t quite scale the previous heights of the novels in the series, and I’d find it hard to argue that it adds a great deal to what we already know of Severian’s tale. As so often in Wolfe there is this persistent sense of an incredibly gifted storyteller who is somehow just winging it — just making it all up as he goes along? Sometimes the effect is remarkable and somehow it’s baffling. Sometimes it’s also a little tedious. Most of the time you just don’t know he is getting away with it. There’s a lot of ponderous pottering in this book — some action, sure — but the tone is muted, abstract. Distracted, you could call it. <br /><br />If you’ve made it all the way through this series so far you may have some idea of whether you’ll have the patience for this or not. The writing is often quite brilliant. But it’s not a book I could easily recommend to anyone except those who will have some idea what they are in for. There is a certain kind of Wolfe fan (i.e. me) for whom whether to read this is not even really a question at all; more so than with any writer I’ve encountered for a long, long time, his work only ever makes me want to read more of it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13618140280392210855" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1913401373" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1913401373" />
    <a title="Chris Gager" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager"><img alt="Chris Gager" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307204529p2/5295008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1913401373?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gager" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gager" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager">Chris Gager</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312930332" href="/book/show/3215235-the-urth-of-the-new-sun">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1913401373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1076373181859372190">I've been a sci-fi reader since the late fifties(Jr. H.S.) and while I wouldn't exactly call myself an aficionado, I have read a lot of sci-fi. But ... I don't recall ever reading any of Gene Wolfe's books. Perhaps a short story or two in anthologies. Maybe he'll be my new Jack Vance/Cordwainer Smith/James Schmitz/Fred Saberhagen discovery. Excellent! The book's off to a great start with a stunning, thrilling and heart-stopping little excursion out in the rigging of a giant sail-type(Cordwainer </span>
  <span id="freeText1076373181859372190" style="display:none">I've been a sci-fi reader since the late fifties(Jr. H.S.) and while I wouldn't exactly call myself an aficionado, I have read a lot of sci-fi. But ... I don't recall ever reading any of Gene Wolfe's books. Perhaps a short story or two in anthologies. Maybe he'll be my new Jack Vance/Cordwainer Smith/James Schmitz/Fred Saberhagen discovery. Excellent! The book's off to a great start with a stunning, thrilling and heart-stopping little excursion out in the rigging of a giant sail-type(Cordwainer Smith calling!) spaceship. And more ...<br /><br />Goodreads recently installed some doo-dads at the bottom of the review. WHY??? does it say I've read this two times already??? Ridiculous ...<br /><br />So anyway, I continued on last night to plow my way into this entertaining and engrossing but hard-to-follow book. It's the way Mr. Wolfe likes to write I guess. I AM enjoying it but there's a lot of murk there. So far I think I can say with confidence that Gene Wolfe will not be replacing the breezy and amusing Jack Vance as my favorite sci-fi writer. Or even Iain M. Banks for that matter. For ONE thing, Mr. Wolfe is a "you see"-er. Continued use of that phrase is a dead give-away that a writer is stuck in 1950's mode.<br /><br />So, I'm not even halfway through this dense and intense story, but it seems like I've read a whole book already. There's very little down time here. The action is fast and furious with plenty of blood and guts and the author doesn't often take time to explain things. Or ... if and when he does it doesn't help that much! Then there's the never ending flow of exotic words and made-up words and who the hell knows which is which. There's not a lot of what you would call humor here, not a lot of lightness. Apparently Mr. Wolfe was deeply into Catholicism and that has undoubtedly influenced his work in spiritual and mystical ways. It's all a bit much for me but the overall effect is still pretty striking, even if I don't really know what the bleep is going on half the time. <br /><br />- This is a 50-50 blend of sci-fi and fantasy.<br /><br />- I did figure out one plot twist ahead of time - it seemed pretty obvious.<br /><br />Our hero has returned to Urth to save it with a new sun and things are much more "down to earth" literally and figuratively. The medieval village setting is a familiar one to sci-fi writers and readers. There's something attractive about it I guess. A question: according to current-day scientists, the "end" of our sun is many millions(H.G. Wells had it at 30,000,000 and was said to be way short) away. Is it realistic for humans, as much as they might be at all recognizable, to still be much the same as we are now? I realize that culture is doing our evolving for us these days, but wouldn't genetic drift, technology and "other factors" over such a long period of time create a very different species from what we are now? Maybe the author dealt with this in the earlier stories ...<br /><br />Things were going along smoothly enough as I approached the end of this up-and-down book. I anticipated finishing it before I went to bed. This I did, but only by kicking into super-skim mode. I could have read it all and finished tonight but by then I was too pissed off and frustrated. I just wanted to put an end to it. What happened? Well, for one thing Mr. Wolfe decided to deploy his obscure word list - BIG TIME! I happen to be a word looker-upper and my nice progress to the end slowed to snail's pace. I was looking up 2-3 words in a single sentence. This is my overview of the "thing" Gene Wolfe wrote: 1) Sevarian rides a huge space-time ship to Yesod with many intense adventures - pretty good stuff, if a bit vague and confusing at times. 2) Sev. passes his trial on Yesod, a kind of heavenly place out there/then somewhere. A bit all over the place and less good, but still palatable and interesting enough, as much as I could figure out. 3) Sev. goes back to Urth on the same ship to bring salvation(are we getting the religious stuff now?) via a gifted new sun, which seems to be tagging along behind him. 4) Sev. lands on Urth and have various Jesus-like adventures(raising the dead etc.) while traveling in a sort-of degraded medieval environment. Where's he going? What's he gonna do? And 5) the never-ending conclusion as the new sun arrives and apocalypse ensues and Sevarian sets out on yet MORE wanderings(and swimmings) having to do with ... ??? Right about there I gave up and started skimming. I suppose that Mr. Wolfe's vision, his gift, got away from him. The story was always threatening to slip into mental/verbal chaos(and the attendant boredom) and to me that's what happened. The man was/is obviously a genius, but prodigious gifts need to be channeled and organized so they can be communicated in reasonably clear and entertaining ways. Mr. Wolfe's spiritual/fantastical/religious "delirium"" got the best of him and his storytelling words. IMHO ...<br /><br />A couple of quotes: "Roaring like the thunder it flung back the double doors that had been propped open by the undine's waist, and it carried that perfume - wild and saline, as fetid and fecund as a woman's groin, that once met can never be forgotten..." Can you say "over the top"??? and ... "'It was late one night, sieur, and my father was making his final rounds when he saw someone attired in the fuligin habiliments of a carnifex ..." - Whuh???<br /><br />- All those weird/archaic words: he uses "destrier" and "fuligin" about ten times apiece.<br /><br />- The deluge - more biblical stuff. It's all there plus the kitchen sink.<br /><br />- Near the end we get not only the flood of water and obscure words, but also of look-backs to people and events of the previous books. DUDE! I haven't read them!<br /><br />- Similarities to "The Time Machine" crop up too.<br /><br />And so to the final rating. This book started out as a 3* - my usual approach - and rose to a 4* quickly. Then it began to settle back to a 3* and went plunging to 2* by the end in the wake of my frustration over the ridiculously prolonged and word-bound ending. I guess I got tired of that Wolfe-thing. At the very end there's a brief "Appendix" by the author(or is it Severian?) that indicates that Mr. Wolfe assumes that we're all supposed to take this stuff seriously - AFTER we've read it! Maybe WHILE we're reading it, but certainly not after. So silly ... and now one more thing. This book began to remind me of "Gravity's Rainbow" and "The Goldfinch," both of which were near-unreadable to me, but beloved by many others. I guess that one's brain just needs to line up at least adequately to "get" certain writers.<br /><br />- Upon further reflection I upped my rating to 2.75* so I can make this one a three star book. The ending was a bit less than one might want but there was too much good stuff before that to ignore so ...</span>
  <a data-text-id="1076373181859372190" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9954250563204107992" style="display:none">If you felt that many things were left unexplained in the previous 4 books "Urth Of The New Sun" will definitely explain them all but on the other hand it will introduce many more puzzles. Anyway if you reached this far by reading and loving the first four books perhaps you already knew it as a possibility. <br /><br />Here, Gene Wolfe tells the tale of now autarch Severian who takes the travel to undergo his ultimate test on Yesod, and while it is a not fictional mythology book as is Tolkien's "The Silmarillion", "Urth Of The New Sun" explains the universe and its cosmology in which Severian lives by delving into the nature of reality and time and space. <br /><br />This is not all that this book is about I am certain you get this, but I would like to comment this aspect because I have been reading about quantum mechanics, cosmology and the nature of conscience and I find it tremendously interesting that Wolfe addresses these topics here not as just words to make us fear but to tie a complete striking theological and cosmological background to the the multi layered story of Severian the saviour and destroyer.<br /><br />On the other hand if you thought that the whole story Severian has been telling us is merely a means to the author convey literary experiments and lucubrate post modern phrases and that the story was a secondary element, you will have it proved here that the story Wolfe told us was more important than how he wrote it so as you can see I am still here, 3 days after reading it all, and I cannot stop thinking about it still uncertain of what to say about it not because ideas lack in my mind but because so many of them outcrop at once that I hardly can comment them all.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jersey9000" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jersey9000" href="/user/show/5239189-jersey9000">jersey9000</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
